###xml 183 184 183 184 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl1">1</xref>
###xml 186 187 186 187 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">2</xref>
###xml 189 190 189 190 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">3</xref>
###xml 192 193 192 193 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 452 459 452 459 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>
###xml 547 548 547 548 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 638 640 638 640 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 694 701 694 701 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>
###xml 704 705 704 705 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">C</italic>
###xml 710 711 710 711 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">D</italic>
###xml 904 911 904 911 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 1022 1029 1022 1029 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 1039 1045 1039 1045 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">trans-</italic>
###xml 1070 1071 1070 1071 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">E</italic>
###xml 1535 1537 1535 1537 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 9 1539 9 1539 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="75"><bold>Schematic representation of the binding of CaM to each of the four individual Cx32-derived peptides examined in this study.</bold> For dissociation kinetic results, refer to Tables <xref ref-type="table" rid="tbl1">1</xref>, <xref ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">2</xref>, <xref ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">3</xref>. <italic>A</italic>, the binding of CaM to Cx32 N-terminal tail 1&#8211;19 peptide. The 1&#8211;19 N-terminal tail may not adopt a fully helical conformation because the lack of residues 20&#8211;21 results in a weak, dynamic interaction of the N-lobe, with only EF1 involved in peptide binding (<xref ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>). With a 1&#8211;19 as N-terminal CaM-binding domain, only the CaM C-lobe would be anchored. <italic>B</italic>, CaM binding to a Cx32 N-terminal 1&#8211;22 tail. A high affinity interaction with all four Ca<sup>2+</sup>-binding sites of CaM is involved in peptide binding (<xref ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>). <italic>C</italic> and <italic>D</italic>, CaM binding to Cx32 C-terminal tail 208&#8211;226/208&#8211;227 peptides. Binding site for only one CaM lobe is present in these peptides; the CaM C-lobe exhibits a higher affinity for the binding site (<xref ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">Table 3</xref>); however, in the absence of the C-lobe, the CaM N-lobe shows moderate affinity for binding via the EF1 site (<xref ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">Table 3</xref>), making <italic>trans-</italic>domain binding feasible. <italic>E</italic>, a representation of how CaM may bind to a whole connexin subunit. Should the Cx32 N-terminal tail be available, it is hypothesized that both lobes of CaM will bind; however, inaccessibility of residues 21 and 22 would result in the destabilization of CaM N-lobe binding and may result in its release. The N- and C-lobes of CaM compete for the C-terminal Cx32 tail-binding site, and engagement of the N-lobe would be especially favorable at high intracellular [Ca<sup>2+</sup>].</p>

FIGURE 7.Schematic representation of the binding of CaM to each of the four individual Cx32-derived peptides examined in this study. For dissociation kinetic results, refer to Tables 1, 2, 3. A, the binding of CaM to Cx32 N-terminal tail 1-19 peptide. The 1-19 N-terminal tail may not adopt a fully helical conformation because the lack of residues 20-21 results in a weak, dynamic interaction of the N-lobe, with only EF1 involved in peptide binding (Table 2). With a 1-19 as N-terminal CaM-binding domain, only the CaM C-lobe would be anchored. B, CaM binding to a Cx32 N-terminal 1-22 tail. A high affinity interaction with all four Ca2+-binding sites of CaM is involved in peptide binding (Table 2). C and D, CaM binding to Cx32 C-terminal tail 208-226/208-227 peptides. Binding site for only one CaM lobe is present in these peptides; the CaM C-lobe exhibits a higher affinity for the binding site (Table 3); however, in the absence of the C-lobe, the CaM N-lobe shows moderate affinity for binding via the EF1 site (Table 3), making trans-domain binding feasible. E, a representation of how CaM may bind to a whole connexin subunit. Should the Cx32 N-terminal tail be available, it is hypothesized that both lobes of CaM will bind; however, inaccessibility of residues 21 and 22 would result in the destabilization of CaM N-lobe binding and may result in its release. The N- and C-lobes of CaM compete for the C-terminal Cx32 tail-binding site, and engagement of the N-lobe would be especially favorable at high intracellular [Ca2+].
###end p 74
###begin p 75
###xml 0 123 0 123 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Schematic representation of the binding of CaM to each of the four individual Cx32-derived peptides examined in this study.</bold>
###xml 174 175 174 175 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl1">1</xref>
###xml 177 178 177 178 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">2</xref>
###xml 180 181 180 181 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">3</xref>
###xml 183 184 183 184 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 443 450 443 450 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>
###xml 538 539 538 539 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</italic>
###xml 629 631 629 631 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 685 692 685 692 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">Table 2</xref>
###xml 695 696 695 696 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">C</italic>
###xml 701 702 701 702 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">D</italic>
###xml 895 902 895 902 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 1013 1020 1013 1020 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 1030 1036 1030 1036 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">trans-</italic>
###xml 1061 1062 1061 1062 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">E</italic>
###xml 1526 1528 1526 1528 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Schematic representation of the binding of CaM to each of the four individual Cx32-derived peptides examined in this study. For dissociation kinetic results, refer to Tables 1, 2, 3. A, the binding of CaM to Cx32 N-terminal tail 1-19 peptide. The 1-19 N-terminal tail may not adopt a fully helical conformation because the lack of residues 20-21 results in a weak, dynamic interaction of the N-lobe, with only EF1 involved in peptide binding (Table 2). With a 1-19 as N-terminal CaM-binding domain, only the CaM C-lobe would be anchored. B, CaM binding to a Cx32 N-terminal 1-22 tail. A high affinity interaction with all four Ca2+-binding sites of CaM is involved in peptide binding (Table 2). C and D, CaM binding to Cx32 C-terminal tail 208-226/208-227 peptides. Binding site for only one CaM lobe is present in these peptides; the CaM C-lobe exhibits a higher affinity for the binding site (Table 3); however, in the absence of the C-lobe, the CaM N-lobe shows moderate affinity for binding via the EF1 site (Table 3), making trans-domain binding feasible. E, a representation of how CaM may bind to a whole connexin subunit. Should the Cx32 N-terminal tail be available, it is hypothesized that both lobes of CaM will bind; however, inaccessibility of residues 21 and 22 would result in the destabilization of CaM N-lobe binding and may result in its release. The N- and C-lobes of CaM compete for the C-terminal Cx32 tail-binding site, and engagement of the N-lobe would be especially favorable at high intracellular [Ca2+].
###end p 75
###begin p 76
###xml 6 7 6 7 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">E</italic>
###xml 0 7 0 7 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig7" ref-type="fig">Fig. 7<italic>E</italic></xref>
###xml 197 204 197 204 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 300 301 300 301 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref5">5</xref>
###xml 565 571 565 571 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">trans-</italic>
###xml 581 587 581 587 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">trans-</italic>
Fig. 7E summarizes the CaM binding modes to a connexin32 molecule, determined by our data. The CaM C-lobe had a substantially higher affinity for the Cx32 C-terminal tail peptides than the N-lobe (Table 3). An unbound CaM lobe has been suggested to act as a "cork" to gate gap junction conductivity (5). At high peptide concentrations, however, a second peptide molecule could attach to the N-lobe, resulting in one CaM molecule binding two separate Cx32 C-terminal peptides. In a gap junction, the local concentration of CaM-binding domains may be high enough for trans-domain or trans-subunit bridging to occur by the two lobes of a CaM molecule.
###end p 76
###begin p 77
###xml 109 111 109 111 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref30">30</xref>
###xml 278 280 278 280 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref31">31</xref>
###xml 282 284 282 284 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref32">32</xref>
###xml 456 458 456 458 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref31">31</xref>
When considering possible mechanisms by which CaM could regulate gap junction conductance, the architecture (30) and properties of the gap junction channel need to be taken into account. Gap junctions show charge selectivity, which is not explained by a simple open pore model (31, 32) but which suggests similarities with ion channels. The open probability of the gap junction channel can decrease without selective decrease in large solute permeability (31). A dynamic interaction model of CaM with gap junctions as outlined above would be consistent with a reduced open probability but unaffected large solute permeability. The significance of CaM binding to the N- and C-terminal binding domains of Cx32 in the regulation of pore permeability and open probability, however, requires further investigation.
###end p 77
###begin p 78
###xml 417 419 417 419 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref29">29</xref>
Several questions remain to be answered to obtain a clear and definitive a model of how CaM may function as a gate for the gap junction channel. First, the stoichiometry of CaM binding to gap junctions is important in understanding the gating mechanisms but has not been determined. Second, it is not clear whether, in the case of connexin32, the N-terminal tail is accessible for CaM binding in assembled junctions (29). Third, CaM appears to interact with different connexins at different regions and by different mechanisms, raising the question of how heteromeric gap junctions may interact with and be regulated by CaM.
###end p 78
###begin p 79
When considering the proximity of CaM-binding domains in connexins to the transmembrane region, an intriguing possibility of regulation emerges: CaM may affect channel open probability without reducing large solute permeability by blocking conformational changes that would involve the rearrangement of transmembrane regions; conformational changes necessary for channel function may not take place.
###end p 79
###begin p 80
It is clear that peptide studies alone are not sufficient to produce a clear understanding of how CaM controls the gating of gap junctions or hemichannels; however, the studies presented here have produced strong evidence toward CaM binding to Cx32 and show that mutants CaM12 and CaM34 can be used in target binding experiments to help assess the role that each individual lobe plays in the binding process. Further investigation is required to achieve a definitive understanding of gap junction gating mechanisms by CaM.
###end p 80
